Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The particular individuation (tashakhs) cannot be predicated on an individual at all because it is a real particular.
Correction: The particular can be predicated in one of the three considerations (lā bi-sharṭ), while in another it is a real part. So predication is possible without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, how many considerations does the universal have in relation to the particular?
A) Two: with and without condition.
B) Three: with condition of inclusion, with condition of exclusion, and without condition.
C) Four: matter, form, genus, species.
D) One: only as predicated.

Key Takeaway
The three considerations of the universal (bi-sharṭ al-dukhūl, bi-sharṭ al-khurūj, lā bi-sharṭ) show how the same entity can be identical to, part of, or predicated on an individual, resolving the apparent contradiction of predicating a particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone as 'just another member of a group', are you considering them under bi-sharṭ al-dukhūl (identifying) or bi-sharṭ al-khurūj (excluding individuality)? How can you practice viewing them la bi-sharṭ – as both a unique individual and a full human being?
?
Which of the three considerations do you most often apply to yourself? Do you see yourself as identical to your roles (bi-sharṭ al-dukhūl), as a collection of parts (bi-sharṭ al-khurūj), or as a whole person not reducible to either? What would change if you actively chose the la bi-sharṭ perspective?
?
The passage shows that the same 'thing' can be considered in different ways with different logical effects. Where in your life are you confusing multiple considerations of a person or situation, leading to contradiction? What step will you take today to hold those considerations together without confusion?

The Three Ways Genus Relates to Species
Commentary on genus taken as united with difference, resulting in species itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how genus can be considered in three ways: as identical to the species when united with the difference, as a part when the difference is excluded, and as a predicate when taken absolutely.

Core Idea
Genus can be the very species, a part, or a predicate—depending on the consideration.

In logic, the relationship between genus and species is not fixed. Depending on how we consider the genus—whether we include or exclude the specific difference—it can take on different logical roles. This lesson explores the first consideration where genus, when taken with the condition of entry of the difference, becomes identical to the species itself. We will also see the other two considerations and their implications for predication and part-hood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three considerations
Grasp the three ways of considering genus: with the condition of entry of the species (bi-sharṭ dukhūl al-nawʻ), with the condition of exclusion (bi-sharṭ khurūj al-nawʻ), and absolutely (lā bi-sharṭ).
These are the key distinctions in this passage.
2
Examine the example of 'animal' and 'rational'
Read how the genus 'animal' when taken as actualized by including 'rational' becomes the species 'human'. Then see how it becomes a part when rational is excluded, and a predicate when considered absolutely.
The example illustrates the three considerations concretely.
3
Read the commentary on the branching
The commentary explains how the three considerations arise from the union and difference between universal and particular. Pay attention to the phrase 'قوله: (فإذا اعتبرنا إلخ)' which branches from the previous discussion.
This commentary clarifies the logical basis.
4
Review Avicenna's threefold distinction
The passage includes a detailed account from al-Shifāʼ that classifies a meaning as genus, matter, or species based on how it receives differences.
This adds depth to the logical analysis.

الحيوان الناطق اتحاده من حيث المفهوم، فإنه خلاف الواقع، بل اعتباره متحصلاً به ومتعيناً، أي صيرورته ناطقاً لا متحصلاً به أمر ثالث؛ كما في المركبات الخارجية. قوله: (قد دخل فيه إلخ حاصله : أن يؤخذ الحيوان متحصلاً تحصلاً نوعياً، بحيث يدخل الناطق في هذا المتحصل، لا الناطق لا بشرط شيء أي الناطق من حيث هو مع قطع النظر عن الإبهام والتحصل، فإنه لا يدخل في النوع، بل الناطق بشرط لا أي باعتبار كونه مغايراً للحيوان خارجاً عنه بأن يعتبر الحيوان المبهم ، ويضم إليه الناطق، فيتحصل كل منهما بالآخر، ويصير نوعاً. وتفصيله ما ذكره الشيخ في الشفاء من أن أي معنى يشكل الحال في جنسيته وماديته، فوجدته قد يجوز معناه يكون جنساً. قوله : (كان هو الإنسان أي من حيث الحقيقة، إذ لا تغاير بين مجموع الحيوان الناطق بالتعبير، كما يعبر أحياناً عن الناطق بمبدئه. وفيه بحث: إذ قد مر أن الجزء الحقيقي ما يحمل على شيء ما، So if we consider 'animal' for example from the aspect that it is 'rational', i.e., from the aspect that it is actualized, having entered into it from this aspect what is apt to actualize it such as 'rational', it is—with the condition of the entry of the species into it and its being actualized corresponding to the complete identity of Zayd—the very same (ʻaynuh). And if it is taken with the condition of the exclusion of the species from it and Zayd being composed of them, it is a part not predicated upon it, and in this consideration it is a true particular (juzʼī ḥaqīqī); because the conjunction of a universal to a universal does not yield individuation. And if it is taken in itself without regard to actualization and indeterminacy, it has two aspects and is predicated upon it, and this does not contradict its being a true particular by virtue of its exclusion from the species and its conjunction with it. His saying: (So if we consider, etc.) is a branching from what preceded, i.e., if between the universal and the particular after conjunction the two aspects of difference and union occur, then if the universal is considered from the aspect of union it is a species, if considered from the aspect of difference it is a part, and if considered disregarding both it is a predicate. So predication is valid along with part-hood due to the difference between part and predicate in consideration, even though they are identical in essence. And the application of 'part' to the essential (dhātī) in our saying 'the predicated parts' is by virtue of its being a part of the definition of the species, or by virtue of its being united with the part in essence. His saying: (i.e., from the aspect that it is actualized) means that what is intended by the union of 'animal' and 'rational' is not their union in concept, for that is contrary to reality, but rather considering it as actualized by it and determined, i.e., its becoming rational not actualized by it is a third matter; as in external composites. His saying: (has entered into it, etc.) its gist: that 'animal' is taken as actualized with a specific actualization, such that 'rational' enters into this actualized, not 'rational' unconditionally (lā bi-sharṭ shayʼ), i.e., 'rational' in itself disregarding indeterminacy and actualization, for it does not enter into the species. Rather, 'rational' with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā), i.e., by virtue of its being different from 'animal' outside it, such that the indeterminate 'animal' is considered and 'rational' is joined to it, so each becomes actualized by the other, and it becomes a species. Its detailed account is what the Shaykh mentioned in al-Shifāʼ: that any meaning that is problematic regarding its genus-hood and materiality—I found that it may allow the conjunction of differences to it. If it is that they are in it and from it, it is a genus; if you take it from the aspect of deficiency of the differences and complete the meaning with it and seal it such that if anything else entered it would not be from that set and would be external, it is not a genus but rather matter; and if you necessitate for it complete meaning such that what can enter enters it, it becomes a species. So then, with the condition that there be no increase, it is matter; with the condition that there be increase, it is a species; and by not attending to that, but allowing each of the increases to be included in the totality of its meaning, it is a genus. His saying: (it was the human) i.e., in terms of reality, for there is no difference between the composite of 'rational animal' as expressed, just as one sometimes expresses 'rational' by its principle. There is a discussion: for it has passed that the real part is what is predicated of something, and it will be mentioned in the discussion of determination that every determined true particular according to the philosophers, so how is it permissible to predicate it of something? The correct view is that what is meant by his saying 'these things were not conceived to be predicated, etc.' with respect to individuation is the validity of considering it on the side of the subject only, so reflect.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the first consideration of genus when it is taken as united with the specific difference. The author begins by taking the example of 'animal' considered as 'rational'. He explains that when animal is taken from the aspect that it is actualized by including the difference, it becomes identical to the species (e.g., human). This is the consideration of genus 'with the condition of the entry of the species' (bi-sharṭ dukhūl al-nawʻ). He then contrasts this with taking genus with the condition of exclusion (bi-sharṭ khurūj al-nawʻ), where it becomes a part not predicated. A third way is taking genus absolutely, disregarding both actualization and indeterminacy, where it has two aspects and can be predicated. The commentary then explains the original text 'قوله: (فإذا اعتبرنا إلخ)' as a branching from the previous discussion. It clarifies that when the universal and particular are conjoined, they have aspects of union (making a species) and difference (making a part). The predication is valid alongside part-hood because the difference is in consideration, not in essence. The author then explains the meaning of 'actualized' (mutaḥaṣṣil): it is not conceptual union but actual determination. He then summarizes the gist of the phrase 'قد دخل فيه' by emphasizing that animal is taken as actualized such that rational enters into it, not as unconditioned. The detailed account from Avicenna's al-Shifāʼ explains the threefold distinction: if the meaning is taken as allowing differences to be in it and from it, it is a genus; if taken as deficient and completed by a difference that seals it, it is matter; if taken as receiving completion by what enters, it is a species. Finally, the commentary addresses a potential objection about predication of the real part, noting that the point is about individuation, not predication in the usual sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that genus and species are always distinct entities.
Correction: Here it is shown that under one consideration (with condition of entry), genus is identical to the species.

Checkpoint
According to the first consideration, when is genus identical to the species?
A) When it is taken with the condition of exclusion of the species.
B) When it is taken absolutely without regard to actualization.
C) When it is taken with the condition of entry of the species into its actualization.
D) When it is taken as a part composing a third.

Key Takeaway
Genus can be considered in three ways: as identical to the species (with entry of difference), as a part (with exclusion of difference), or as a predicate (absolutely).

?
Consider the idea that conjunction of universals does not yield individuation. What does this say about your own unique existence? Are you content to be defined by universal categories, or do you seek the true particularity that only Allah can give?

Second and Third Considerations of Genus
Al-Ījī explains the second and third considerations of genus and their predication rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third considerations of genus and how they affect predication.

Core Idea
Genus taken as a separate part differs from genus taken as an absolute predicate.

The study of genus involves more than one way of considering it. Al-Ījī explains the second and third considerations: when genus is taken as a distinct concept from the difference, and when it is taken absolutely. These considerations determine whether genus can be predicated of the species or not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second consideration
Read the passage where genus is taken as a concept other than the difference, conjoined to it. Notice that a composite essence results, and each becomes a part.
This consideration treats genus and difference as separate parts.
2
Understand the predication rule
In this consideration, nothing is predicated of the other nor of the composite. Ask yourself why.
3
Contrast with the third consideration
Now read the third consideration: taking genus as itself, without any condition. Here it becomes the predicate of the species.
This is the absolute (lā bi-sharṭ) consideration.
4
Connect to the summary
The passage ends with the beginning of the summary of the three ways. This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وإذا من حيث هو هو من غير اعتبار أنه ناطق بوجه كما أخذناه أولاً ، ( أو غيره بوجه كما أخذناه ثانياً، فهو المحمول على الإنسان والحاصل أن الأجزاء المتمايزة بحسب العقل دون الخارج لها اعتبارات : فإن الصورة العقلية تؤخذ تارة بشرط شيء أي بشرط أن ينضم إليها صورة أخرى فيطابقان معاً أمراً واحداً، فلا يلاحظ حينئذ تغايرهما بل اتحادهما . كالحيوان والناطق المأخوذين من حيث إنهما يطابقان الماهية الإنسانية فالجنس المأخوذ بهذا الاعتبار هو عين النوع، وكذا النوع، وكذا الفصل، وتؤخذ تارة بشرط لا شيء أي بشرط أنها صورة على حدة بحيث إذا انضمت إلى صورة أخرى كانتا متغايرتين وقد تركب منهما ماهية ثالثة كالحيوان والناطق إذا والحيوان المحصل بالناطق، وإن كانا متغايرين في المفهوم ضرورة أن مفهوم الحيوان المحصل غير مفهوم المجموع، وهذا معنى قول الشارح قدس سره : إذ لا معنى للإنسان إلخ . قوله : ( وإذا أخذناه إلخ) أي أخذنا كل واحد منهما مفهوماً مغايراً للآخر يتحصل منهما أمر ثالث كما في المركبات الخارجية . قوله : (لا يحمل شيء منهما على الآخر) لأنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين، ولا على المركب؛ لأنه حكم بوحدة الجزء مع الكل. قوله : أن ينضم إليها صورة أخرى بحيث تكون محصلة لها ومعينة إياها، وهذا معنى دخولها فيها وكونها إياها، ومنضمة فيه على ما وقع في العبارات لا من حيث أن تكون محصلة لأمر ثالث كما في الاعتبار الثاني، فتتحد إحداهما بالأخرى في هذا الاعتبار ضرورة أن الحيوان المحصل هو الناطق المحصل، فيطابقان معاً أمراً واحداً ، أي يكونان حينئذ صورة واحدة مرآة لمشاهدة أمر واحد هو النوع لا اختلاف بينهما إلا من حيث القيام بالذهن وعدمه . قوله : ( صورة على حدة) أي لا يعتبر كونها محصلة لتلك الصورة، بل من حيث إنها بانضمامها إلى الأخرى محصلة لثالث . قوله : (أي بشرط أن ينضم إليها صورة أخرى وتلك الأخرى هي الفصل، كما هو الظاهر أو الجنس : فظهر أن هذا غير المأخوذ بشرط شيء الذي سبق ذكره فإنه أعم . قوله : ( وكذا الفصل) نقل عنه أنه يمكن فيه تلك الاعتبارات إلا أنها بالنسبة إلى الجنس أولى؛ لأنه بمنزلة المادة . قوله : (أي بشرط أنها (صورة فظهر أنه غير المأخوذ بشرط لا شيء الذي سبق .
And if we take it from the aspect that it is a concept other than it, i.e., other than 'rational', conjoined to it, i.e., to 'rational', there results from them a composite essence that is other than them, each one of them becomes a part of it, i.e., of that essence. And in this consideration nothing is predicated of the other nor of the composite essence from them. (And if [we take it] from the aspect that it is itself without considering that it is 'rational' in any way as we took it first, or other than it in any way as we took it second, then it is the predicate of 'human'. And the conclusion is that the parts distinguished by the intellect and not externally have considerations: for the mental form is taken sometimes with the condition of something (bi-sharṭ shayʼ), i.e., with the condition that another form be conjoined to it so that together they correspond to one single thing, and then their difference is not observed but rather their union. Like 'animal' and 'rational' taken from the aspect that they correspond to the human essence, so the genus taken in this consideration is the very species, and likewise the species, and likewise the difference. And it is taken sometimes with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā shayʼ), i.e., with the condition that it is a separate form such that when it is conjoined to another form they become two different things and a third essence may be composed from them, like 'animal' and 'rational' when... and 'animal' actualized by 'rational', even if they are different in concept necessarily, for the concept of 'animal' actualized is not the concept of the composite. And this is the meaning of the commentator's saying: 'for there is no meaning for human except...' His saying: (And if we take it, etc.) means we take each one of them as a concept distinct from the other, from which a third thing is actualized as in external composites. His saying: (Nothing is predicated of the other) because it is a judgment of the union of the two, nor of the composite because it is a judgment of the union of the part with the whole. His saying: that another form be conjoined to it such that it is actualizing it and determining it; this is the meaning of its entering into it and being it, and being conjoined in it as occurs in expressions, not from the aspect that it is actualizing a third thing as in the second consideration. So one of them unites with the other in this consideration necessarily, for 'animal' actualized is 'rational' actualized, so they both correspond to one single thing, i.e., they become then one single form mirroring the observation of one single thing which is the species; there is no difference between them except in terms of mental existence and its absence. His saying: (a separate form) means it is not considered as actualizing that form, but rather from the aspect that by its conjunction with the other it actualizes a third. His saying: (i.e., with the condition that another form be conjoined to it and that other is the difference, as is apparent, or the genus: so it becomes clear that this is different from the 'taken with the condition of something' that preceded, for that is more general. His saying: (And likewise the difference) reported from him that those considerations are possible for it, except that they are more appropriate for the genus because it is like matter. His saying: (i.e., with the condition that it is a form) so it becomes clear that it is different from the 'taken with the condition of nothing' that preceded.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the second and third considerations of genus and then provides a summary of the three ways of taking mental forms. The second consideration: if genus is taken as a distinct concept separate from the difference, and they are conjoined, they form a composite essence that is other than each, and each becomes a part of that composite. In this case, nothing is predicated of the other or of the composite. The third consideration: if genus is taken absolutely, without considering it as either identical to or different from the difference, it is predicated of the species (e.g., 'human'). The summary (al-ḥāṣil) states that parts that are distinct intellectually (not externally) have three considerations: (1) taken with the condition of something (bi-sharṭ shayʼ), i.e., another form conjoined to it such that they correspond to one thing; here genus, species, and difference are identical. (2) taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā shayʼ), i.e., as a separate form such that when conjoined to another, they produce a third; here they are distinct parts. (3) taken absolutely (lā bi-sharṭ), i.e., without any condition; this corresponds to the third consideration above. The commentary then explains each phrase: the second consideration is like external composites; the third consideration involves a form that actualizes and determines the other, leading to unity in the thing observed. The commentary clarifies that the phrase 'with the condition of something' here is more specific than the earlier one, and that the difference can also be considered in these three ways, but the genus is more appropriate for such analysis because it is like matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that genus is always predicated of the species in the same way.
Correction: Genus is predicated of the species only when taken absolutely; when taken as a separate part in a composite, it is not predicated.

Checkpoint
In the second consideration of genus, what is the result when genus and difference are taken as distinct concepts conjoined?
A) They are predicated of each other.
B) They become identical to the species.
C) They form a composite essence different from each, and each becomes a part.
D) They are taken as unconditional predicates.

Key Takeaway
The way genus is considered (as a distinct part vs. absolutely) determines its predication status regarding the species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study theological definitions, have you ever confused the way a term is being considered (as part of a definition or as an absolute predicate)? How might this oversight lead you to misinterpret a foundational belief about Allah's attributes? Commit to reviewing each definition's 'consideration' before drawing conclusions.
?
Think of a religious concept you once misunderstood because you treated its components as separate parts instead of seeing them as unified. How does this lesson's distinction between the second and third considerations of genus challenge you to re-examine your approach? What specific change will you make in your study habits starting today?

The Three Considerations of Mental Forms
The author enumerates the three considerations of mental forms: with condition, without condition, and absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways of taking mental forms: with a condition, without a condition, and absolutely.

Core Idea
Mental forms can be considered in three ways, each yielding different logical roles.

After explaining the second and third considerations of genus, the author summarizes the three fundamental ways a mental form can be taken. These three considerations—bisharṭ shayʼ, bisharṭ lā shayʼ, and lā bisharṭ—are essential in logic for understanding how parts relate to wholes and how predication works.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary introduction
The passage begins with 'al-ḥāṣil' stating that parts distinguished by intellect have considerations. Understand that these are not external parts but mental distinctions.
2
First consideration: bi-sharṭ shayʼ
The mental form is taken with the condition that another form is conjoined to it, so they correspond to one thing. Here genus, species, and difference are identical.
Example: 'animal' and 'rational' taken as both corresponding to 'human'.
3
Second consideration: bi-sharṭ lā shayʼ
The form is taken as separate, so when conjoined with another, they remain distinct and produce a third composite. Here genus and difference are distinct parts.
This matches the second consideration earlier.
4
Third consideration: lā bi-sharṭ
The form is taken without any condition, i.e., absolutely. This is the third consideration where genus is predicated of the species.
5
Note the commentary on applicability to difference
The commentator mentions that these considerations also apply to the difference, but they are more appropriate for genus because genus is like matter.
This clarifies that the categories are not limited to genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أخذناه من حيث هو مفهوم غيره ) ، أي غير الناطق ( منضم إليه ) أي إلى الناطق ( حصلت منهما ماهية مركبة هي غيرهما، كان كل واحد منهما جزءا لها أي لتلك الماهية. وبهذا الاعتبار لا يحمل شيء منهما على الآخر ولا على الماهية المركبة منهما . ( وإذا من حيث هو هو من غير اعتبار أنه ناطق بوجه كما أخذناه أولاً ، ( أو غيره بوجه كما أخذناه ثانياً، فهو المحمول على الإنسان والحاصل أن الأجزاء المتمايزة بحسب العقل دون الخارج لها اعتبارات : فإن الصورة العقلية تؤخذ تارة بشرط شيء أي بشرط أن ينضم إليها صورة أخرى فيطابقان معاً أمراً واحداً، فلا يلاحظ حينئذ تغايرهما بل اتحادهما . كالحيوان والناطق المأخوذين من حيث إنهما يطابقان الماهية الإنسانية فالجنس المأخوذ بهذا الاعتبار هو عين النوع، وكذا النوع، وكذا الفصل، وتؤخذ تارة بشرط لا شيء أي بشرط أنها صورة على حدة بحيث إذا انضمت إلى صورة أخرى كانتا متغايرتين وقد تركب منهما ماهية ثالثة كالحيوان والناطق إذا والحيوان المحصل بالناطق، وإن كانا متغايرين في المفهوم ضرورة أن مفهوم الحيوان المحصل غير مفهوم المجموع، وهذا معنى قول الشارح قدس سره : إذ لا معنى للإنسان إلخ . قوله : ( وإذا أخذناه إلخ) أي أخذنا كل واحد منهما مفهوماً مغايراً للآخر يتحصل منهما أمر ثالث كما في المركبات الخارجية . قوله : (لا يحمل شيء منهما على الآخر) لأنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين، ولا على المركب؛ لأنه حكم بوحدة الجزء مع الكل. قوله : أن ينضم إليها صورة أخرى بحيث تكون محصلة لها ومعينة إياها، وهذا معنى دخولها فيها وكونها إياها، ومنضمة فيه على ما وقع في العبارات لا من حيث أن تكون محصلة لأمر ثالث كما في الاعتبار الثاني، فتتحد إحداهما بالأخرى في هذا الاعتبار ضرورة أن الحيوان المحصل هو الناطق المحصل، فيطابقان معاً أمراً واحداً ، أي يكونان حينئذ صورة واحدة مرآة لمشاهدة أمر واحد هو النوع لا اختلاف بينهما إلا من حيث القيام بالذهن وعدمه . قوله : ( صورة على حدة) أي لا يعتبر كونها محصلة لتلك الصورة، بل من حيث إنها بانضمامها إلى الأخرى محصلة لثالث . قوله : (أي بشرط أن ينضم إليها صورة أخرى وتلك الأخرى هي الفصل، كما هو الظاهر أو الجنس : فظهر أن هذا غير المأخوذ بشرط شيء الذي سبق ذكره فإنه أعم . قوله : ( وكذا الفصل) نقل عنه أنه يمكن فيه تلك الاعتبارات إلا أنها بالنسبة إلى الجنس أولى؛ لأنه بمنزلة المادة . قوله : (أي بشرط أنها (صورة فظهر أنه غير المأخوذ بشرط لا شيء الذي سبق .
And if we take it from the aspect that it is a concept other than it, i.e., other than 'rational', conjoined to it, i.e., to 'rational', there results from them a composite essence that is other than them, each one of them becomes a part of it, i.e., of that essence. And in this consideration nothing is predicated of the other nor of the composite essence from them. (And if [we take it] from the aspect that it is itself without considering that it is 'rational' in any way as we took it first, or other than it in any way as we took it second, then it is the predicate of 'human'. And the conclusion is that the parts distinguished by the intellect and not externally have considerations: for the mental form is taken sometimes with the condition of something (bi-sharṭ shayʼ), i.e., with the condition that another form be conjoined to it so that together they correspond to one single thing, and then their difference is not observed but rather their union. Like 'animal' and 'rational' taken from the aspect that they correspond to the human essence, so the genus taken in this consideration is the very species, and likewise the species, and likewise the difference. And it is taken sometimes with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā shayʼ), i.e., with the condition that it is a separate form such that when it is conjoined to another form they become two different things and a third essence may be composed from them, like 'animal' and 'rational' when... and 'animal' actualized by 'rational', even if they are different in concept necessarily, for the concept of 'animal' actualized is not the concept of the composite. And this is the meaning of the commentator's saying: 'for there is no meaning for human except...' His saying: (And if we take it, etc.) means we take each one of them as a concept distinct from the other, from which a third thing is actualized as in external composites. His saying: (Nothing is predicated of the other) because it is a judgment of the union of the two, nor of the composite because it is a judgment of the union of the part with the whole. His saying: that another form be conjoined to it such that it is actualizing it and determining it; this is the meaning of its entering into it and being it, and being conjoined in it as occurs in expressions, not from the aspect that it is actualizing a third thing as in the second consideration. So one of them unites with the other in this consideration necessarily, for 'animal' actualized is 'rational' actualized, so they both correspond to one single thing, i.e., they become then one single form mirroring the observation of one single thing which is the species; there is no difference between them except in terms of mental existence and its absence. His saying: (a separate form) means it is not considered as actualizing that form, but rather from the aspect that by its conjunction with the other it actualizes a third. His saying: (i.e., with the condition that another form be conjoined to it and that other is the difference, as is apparent, or the genus: so it becomes clear that this is different from the 'taken with the condition of something' that preceded, for that is more general. His saying: (And likewise the difference) reported from him that those considerations are possible for it, except that they are more appropriate for the genus because it is like matter. His saying: (i.e., with the condition that it is a form) so it becomes clear that it is different from the 'taken with the condition of nothing' that preceded.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the second and third considerations of genus and then provides a summary of the three ways of taking mental forms. The second consideration: if genus is taken as a distinct concept separate from the difference, and they are conjoined, they form a composite essence that is other than each, and each becomes a part of that composite. In this case, nothing is predicated of the other or of the composite. The third consideration: if genus is taken absolutely, without considering it as either identical to or different from the difference, it is predicated of the species (e.g., 'human'). The summary (al-ḥāṣil) states that parts that are distinct intellectually (not externally) have three considerations: (1) taken with the condition of something (bi-sharṭ shayʼ), i.e., another form conjoined to it such that they correspond to one thing; here genus, species, and difference are identical. (2) taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā shayʼ), i.e., as a separate form such that when conjoined to another, they produce a third; here they are distinct parts. (3) taken absolutely (lā bi-sharṭ), i.e., without any condition; this corresponds to the third consideration above. The commentary then explains each phrase: the second consideration is like external composites; the third consideration involves a form that actualizes and determines the other, leading to unity in the thing observed. The commentary clarifies that the phrase 'with the condition of something' here is more specific than the earlier one, and that the difference can also be considered in these three ways, but the genus is more appropriate for such analysis because it is like matter.

بشرط لا شيء
لا بشرط
صورة عقلية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the three considerations only apply to genus.
Correction: The commentary states that these considerations also apply to the difference, though they are more appropriate for genus.

Checkpoint
In the first consideration (bi-sharṭ shayʼ), what is the relationship between genus and species?
A) Genus is a part of the species.
B) Genus is identical to the species.
C) Genus is a separate form causing a composite.
D) Genus is the predicate of the species.

Key Takeaway
Mental forms can be considered with a condition of union, with a condition of separation, or absolutely, each determining their logical behavior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you conceive of a complex reality, like the nature of a human being, do you tend to see it as a unified whole or as a composite of parts? How does that mental choice affect the clarity of your understanding and your ability to reason about it? Commit today to practicing the three considerations in your own thinking, especially when studying matters of faith.
?
Consider how you mentally conceive of the attributes of Allah. Do you sometimes forget that they are identical with His essence? Which of the three considerations might lead you to mistakenly treat them as separate parts? Repent of any conceptual error and resolve to use the 'with condition of something' consideration to maintain tawḥīd in your understanding of the divine attributes.
?
In your personal reflections, do you ever find yourself shifting between these considerations unconsciously, leading to confusion or contradiction? How can you train yourself to be aware of which consideration you are using, especially when discussing theological matters with others? Write down one practical step you will take this week to apply this awareness.

The Three Considerations of Intellectual Parts in Composition
Explains the three i'tibārāt of conceptual parts and their role in quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways (i'tibārāt) in which intellectually distinct parts like genus and differentia can be considered when composing a quiddity, and how each consideration affects predication.

Core Idea
Intellectual parts have three considerations: with condition, with condition of nothing, and without condition.

In logic, when we analyze the components of a concept (like 'human' as animal + rational), these components are not physically separate but are distinguished by the mind. How we consider them—whether as united in correspondence, as separate building blocks, or as having both aspects—determines whether they can be predicated of each other or of the composite. This lesson unpacks the three i'tibārāt that govern such distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the examples of 'human' and how the author gradually introduces the three considerations.
The passage begins with a specific example before stating the general rule.
2
Identify the three i'tibārāt
Look for the three terms: بشرط شيء, بشرط لا شيء, لا بشرط شيء. Understand how each one treats the relationship between genus and differentia.
3
Connect each consideration to predication
Notice which consideration allows parts to be predicated of each other (lā bisharṭ shay’) and which do not (bisharṭ shay’ and bisharṭ lā shay’).
The third consideration is called 'the essential predicate' (al-dhātī al-maḥmūl).
4
Reflect on the broader importance
Think about why this distinction matters: it helps clarify how universal concepts relate to each other in definitions and compositions.
This is foundational for understanding genus, species, and differentia in Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد العاشر : وجوب حاجة بعض الأجزاء إلى بعض أخذناه الإنسان)؛ إذ لا معنى للإنسان إلا حيوان دخل في طبيعته الناطق، ( وإذا أخذناه من حيث هو مفهوم غيره ) ، أي غير الناطق ( منضم إليه ) أي إلى الناطق ( حصلت منهما ماهية مركبة هي غيرهما، كان كل واحد منهما جزءا لها أي لتلك الماهية. وبهذا الاعتبار لا يحمل شيء منهما على الآخر ولا على الماهية المركبة منهما . ( وإذا من حيث هو هو من غير اعتبار أنه ناطق بوجه كما أخذناه أولاً ، ( أو غيره بوجه كما أخذناه ثانياً، فهو المحمول على الإنسان والحاصل أن الأجزاء المتمايزة بحسب العقل دون الخارج لها اعتبارات : فإن الصورة العقلية تؤخذ تارة بشرط شيء أي بشرط أن ينضم إليها صورة أخرى فيطابقان معاً أمراً واحداً، فلا يلاحظ حينئذ تغايرهما بل اتحادهما . كالحيوان والناطق المأخوذين من حيث إنهما يطابقان الماهية الإنسانية فالجنس المأخوذ بهذا الاعتبار هو عين النوع، وكذا النوع، وكذا الفصل، وتؤخذ تارة بشرط لا شيء أي بشرط أنها صورة على حدة بحيث إذا انضمت إلى صورة أخرى كانتا متغايرتين وقد تركب منهما ماهية ثالثة كالحيوان والناطق إذا اعتبرا موجودين متغايرين في العقل وقد تركب منهما ماهية الإنسان فكل واحد من الجنس والفصل بهذا الاعتبار جزء ومادة للنوع، فلا يحمل بعضها على بعض . وقد تؤخذ لا بشرط شيء، فيكون لها جهتان؛ إذ يمكن أن يعتبر التغاير بينها وبين ما يقارنها، وأن يعتبر اتحادهما بحسب المطابقة لماهية واحدة. وهذا هو الذاتي المحمول.
The second observatory - the tenth purpose: The necessity of the need of some parts for others. We took it) there is no meaning of 'human' except an animal into whose nature the rational (al-nāṭiq) has entered; (And if we take it insofar as it is a concept of something else), i.e., other than the rational (al-nāṭiq), (joined to it), i.e., to the rational, then a composite quiddity (māhīyah murakkabah) results from them which is other than them, and each one of them is a part for it, i.e., for that quiddity. In this consideration, none of them is predicated of the other nor of the composite quiddity composed of them. (And if [we take it] insofar as it is itself without considering that it is rational in any respect as we took it first, (or something else in any respect as we took it second, then it is what is predicated of the human). The conclusion is that parts distinguished by the intellect (al-‘aql) but not externally have considerations (i‘tibārāt). For the intellectual form (al-ṣūrah al-‘aqlīyah) is taken sometimes with a condition (bisharṭ shay’), meaning with the condition that another form is joined to it so that they both together correspond to one thing; then their duality is not observed but rather their unity. Like animal and rational taken insofar as they both correspond to the human quiddity, so the genus taken in this consideration is identical to the species, and likewise the species, and likewise the differentia. And sometimes it is taken with the condition of nothing (bisharṭ lā shay’), meaning with the condition that it is a form separately, such that when joined to another form they become two distinct things and a third quiddity is composed from them, like animal and rational when they are considered as two distinct existents in the intellect and the human quiddity is composed from them; so each of the genus and differentia in this consideration is a part and matter for the species; thus none of them is predicated of the other. And sometimes it is taken without condition (lā bisharṭ shay’), then it has two aspects; for it is possible to consider the distinction between it and what accompanies it, and to consider their unity in terms of correspondence to a single quiddity. And this is the essential predicated (al-dhātī al-maḥmūl).
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three ways (i‘tibārāt) in which intellectual parts (like genus and differentia) can be considered in the composition of quiddity. The author first describes two examples of how 'human' (al-insān) can be taken: first, as a concept that includes both animal and rational, where each part is not predicated separately; second, as a concept that is simply itself without considering its parts, which is what is predicated. Then he states the general rule: parts distinguished by reason alone have three considerations. (1) Bisharṭ shay’: taken with the condition of joining another form to correspond to one thing—here the two are identical in correspondence, so genus is identical to species. (2) Bisharṭ lā shay’: taken as separate forms that together compose a third—here each is a distinct part and matter for the composite, so they are not predicated of each other. (3) Lā bisharṭ shay’: taken without any condition, allowing both aspects of distinction and unity—this is the essential predicate (al-dhātī al-maḥmūl). The passage follows the author's order, starting with examples, then the classification of three considerations, and concluding with the third as the basis for predication.

Key Terms
اعتبار i‘tibār
بشرط شيء bisharṭ shay’
بشرط لا شيء bisharṭ lā shay’
لا بشرط شيء lā bisharṭ shay’
جنس jins
فصل faṣl
نوع naw‘
ذاتي محمول dhātī maḥmūl

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third consideration (lā bisharṭ shay’) is a separate part or a fourth type of part.
Correction: It is not a separate part but a perspective on the same part, allowing both unity and distinction to be considered.

Checkpoint
In the consideration bisharṭ shay’, how are genus and differentia related?
A) They are distinct and compose a third quiddity
B) They are identical in correspondence to one quiddity
C) They cannot be predicated at all
D) They are considered without any condition

Key Takeaway
The three i'tibārāt—bisharṭ shay’, bisharṭ lā shay’, and lā bisharṭ shay’—determine how intellectually distinct parts relate to each other and to the composite quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the same intellectual parts can be viewed in three different ways, each revealing a different relationship. In your own life, when you think about your own identity—such as being a Muslim, a spouse, a parent—do you tend to fix on only one 'consideration' (e.g., seeing roles as separate parts or as fully merged)? How might applying the 'without condition' perspective help you see both the distinct aspects and their unified purpose without contradiction? What would you need to change in your thinking to achieve this balance?
?
The passage shows that confusing these considerations leads to errors in predication and understanding. Think of a belief you hold about yourself or about God that might be partially true but incomplete because you are only looking from one 'i'tibār'. Are you willing to examine that belief from the other two perspectives, even if it unsettles you? What would it cost you to see it differently?
?
The 'essential predicate' (dhātī maḥmūl) is what is predicated without condition. Is there an aspect of your faith—like the oneness of God (tawḥīd)—that you tend to qualify with conditions or exceptions in your daily actions? How can you train yourself to affirm it 'without condition' in your heart and deeds, even when your mind struggles with the implications?

The Meaning of Predication: Unity of Referent, Not Concept
Defines predication as unity of referent despite conceptual difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that predication (ḥaml) is based on the unity of the external or estimative identity of the subject and predicate, not on the identity of their concepts.

Core Idea
Predication unites two different concepts in a single referent.

In logic, when we say 'The human is an animal', we are not claiming that 'human' and 'animal' are the same concept. They are distinct. So how can one thing be predicated of another? This lesson unpacks the classical solution: predication refers to a single external or mental identity that underlies the two concepts, thereby avoiding the contradiction of either making two things one or saying something trivial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Read the passage and notice the two problematic scenarios: if the predicate is different from the subject, predication would assert unity of two distinct things; if identical, it would be trivial self-predication.
Keep in mind the objection that predication seems either false or meaningless.
2
Grasp the Solution
The author introduces the concept of 'huwīyah' (identity). The two concepts are distinct in the mind, but their referent is one externally or hypothetically.
This single identity resolves the apparent contradiction.
3
Consider the Cases
External identity applies to real entities; estimative identity covers hypothetical or impossible things. Understand how each works.
Examples: 'The phoenix is a bird' has estimative identity.
4
Reflect on the Limitation
This analysis is specific to certain types of predication; it does not allow predicating a true particular of a universal, which is generally disallowed.
The definition given is an interpretation by specific property, not the full reality of predication.

ومعنى (حمله أي حمل الحيوان مثلا ( عليه ) أي على الإنسان (أن هذين المفهومين المتغايرين في العقل هويتهما الخارجية أو الوهمية واحدة)، فلا تلزم وحدة الاثنين، ولا حمل الشيء على نفسه يعني : قد اندفع بما حققناه من معنى الحمل ما يقال من أن المحمول إن كان غير الموضوع يلزم من الحمل بالمواطأة : الحكم بوحدة الاثنين، وإن كان عينه يلزم حمل الشيء على نفسه، فلا يكون مفيداً، بل لا يكون هناك حمل حقيقي. وهذا المقام يستدعي مزيد بسط في الكلام لينضبط به المرام، وهو أن تقول : لا إشكال في تركب الماهية من الأجزاء الخارجية التي لا قوله : ( ومادة للنوع ) يشعر بأن الفصل بشرط لا شيء يطلق عليه المادة كالجنس، ووقع في عبارة الشيخ إطلاق الصورة عليه، ولعل ذلك باعتبارين مختلفين إن لوحظ كونه أخص من الجنس فهو صورة، وإن لوحظ كون كل واحد منهما أعم من الآخر من حيث المفهوم كان كل واحد منهما مادة وصورة . قوله : ( ومعني حمله إلخ) لما بين جهة الحمل في الأجزاء المحمولة ساق الكلام في بيان معنى الحمل تتميما للمرام. قوله : ( هويتهما الخارجية) أي ماهيتهما الشخصية الثابتة في نفس الأمر، سواء كان في الأعيان أو في الأذهان فيشمل القضايا الخارجية والحقيقية والذهنية التي أفرادها من الموجودات الذهنية . قوله : (أو الوهمية أي الفرضية، فيشمل مثل قولنا : شريك الباري ممتنع والعنقاء طائر، ونحو ذلك مما أفرادها فرضية محضة . قوله : ( حقيقي ) بل في اللفظ فقط . قوله : ( في تركب الماهية إلخ) ما مر كان بياناً لكيفية الحمل، وهذا بيان لكيفية التركب منها، هل هو في الذهن فقط، أو في الخارج أيضاً، ثم إنه قبل اتصافها بالوجود في الخارج، أو قوله : ( فلا يحمل بعضها على بعض فإن الحيوان الذي لا يكون معه الناطق، أي لا يدخل مسلوب عن الإنسان، فاستحال حمله عليه كذا في حواشي حكمة العين . قوله : ( ومعنى إلخ) المشهور عدم جواز حمل الجزئي الحقيقي على الكلي، فليس هذا المذكور حقيقة الحمل، وإلا لجاز حمله عليه بل هو تفسير له بخاصته ولو إضافية، كذا أفاده الأستاذ المحقق . قوله : ( أو الوهمية كما في الماهيات المركبة الفرضية .

Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the meaning of predication (ḥaml) and a resolution of a typical problem. The author begins by stating the meaning of predication (e.g., 'animal is predicated of human'): the two concepts are distinct in the intellect, but their external or estimative identity is one. This avoids two problems: (a) if the predicate were entirely other than the subject, predication would assert the unity of two distinct things; (b) if it were identical, predication would be of a thing upon itself, which is trivial. By clarifying that the referent (al-huwīyah) is single while the concepts differ, the objection is refuted. The passage then includes further commentary by the author (or the glossator) elaborating on key terms: 'external identity' includes both real and mental individuals; 'estimative' covers hypothetical cases like impossible or imaginary entities. It also discusses the nature of the differentia as matter or form depending on perspective, and notes that the well-known position forbids predicating a true particular of a universal, so the given explanation is a specific interpretation of predication, not its full reality.

خارجية khārijīyah
وهمية wahmīyah
ذاتي dhātī
عرضī 'araḍī

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Predication requires that the predicate and subject have the same meaning or concept.
Correction: Predication requires only that their referent (identity) is one, even if their concepts differ.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the key to resolving the problem of predication?
A) The predicate must be more general than the subject
B) The identity of the external or estimative referent despite conceptual difference
C) The predicate and subject must be identical in meaning
D) Predication is only verbal, not real

Key Takeaway
Predication resolves the duality of concept and reality by asserting a single identity behind distinct concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a recent disagreement that turned on words. Could it be that you and the other person were using different concepts to point to the same reality? What would change if you assumed unity of referent before insisting on your own concept?
?
You have many descriptions of yourself—your roles, your sins, your deeds. Do you believe they all refer to one person who will stand before Allah? Or do you sometimes fragment yourself to avoid responsibility? How will you practice living as one unified self today?

Essential Predication and the Refutation of Circularity
The commentator explains the meaning of essential predication and refutes an objection of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of essential predication (al-dhati al-mahmul) and how the objection of circularity or self-predication is resolved.

Core Idea
Essential predication unites distinct concepts in one external identity.

In this passage, the commentator explains a subtle point in logic: how genus and differentia, though distinct in the mind, can be predicated of each other without contradiction. He then addresses a potential objection that either requires unifying two distinct things or leads to self-predication. This clarification is crucial for understanding the logical structure of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial scenario
Read the first part where genus and differentia are considered as distinct mental existents composing a quiddity. Note that in this consideration they are parts, not predicated.
This sets the baseline for the discussion.
2
Grasp the two aspects without condition
When taken without condition, the same concepts can be viewed as distinct or as unified by reference to a single quiddity. The unified perspective is the essential predicated.
This dual aspect is key to resolving the objection.
3
See the refutation of the objection
The objection claims predication either conflates two things or is self-predication. The commentator shows that the predicate and subject are identical in external reality but distinct in the mind, so neither problem arises.
The resolution hinges on the distinction between mental and external identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتبرا موجودين متغايرين في العقل وقد تركب منهما ماهية الإنسان فكل واحد من الجنس والفصل بهذا الاعتبار جزء ومادة للنوع، فلا يحمل بعضها على بعض. وقد تؤخذ لا بشرط شيء، فيكون لها جهتان؛ إذ يمكن أن يعتبر التغاير بينها وبين ما يقارنها، وأن يعتبر اتحادهما بحسب المطابقة لماهية واحدة. وهذا هو الذاتي المحمول. ومعنى حمله أي حمل الحيوان مثلا عليه أي على الإنسان أن هذين المفهومين المتغايرين في العقل هويتهما الخارجية أو الوهمية واحدة، فلا تلزم وحدة الاثنين، ولا حمل الشيء على نفسه يعني: قد اندفع بما حققناه من معنى الحمل ما يقال من أن المحمول إن كان غير الموضوع يلزم من الحمل بالمواطأة: الحكم بوحدة الاثنين، وإن كان عينه يلزم حمل الشيء على نفسه، فلا يكون مفيداً، بل لا يكون هناك حمل حقيقي.
They are considered as two existents distinct in the mind, and from them the quiddity of man is composed. So each one of the genus and differentia, in this consideration, is a part and matter for the species; thus one is not predicated of the other. And sometimes they are taken without condition, so they have two aspects: for it is possible to consider the distinction between them and what accompanies them, and to consider their unity by conformity to one quiddity. And this is the essential predicated. The meaning of its predication—i.e., predication of 'animal' for example, of him—i.e., of man—is that these two concepts, distinct in the mind, have one external or estimative identity. So neither the unity of two things is necessitated, nor the predication of a thing of itself. That is: there has been repelled, by what we have established of the meaning of predication, what is said: that if the predicate is other than the subject, predication by correspondence necessitates the judgment of the unity of the two, and if it is identical to it, it necessitates predication of a thing of itself, so it would not be beneficial; rather, there would be no real predication.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of predication of essential parts (genus and differentia) and refutes an objection. The author begins by stating that when genus and differentia are considered as two distinct mental existents that compose the quiddity of man, each is a part and matter for the species, so one is not predicated of the other. However, if they are taken without condition (i.e., not considering their distinctness), they have two aspects: one can consider them as distinct or as unified in accordance with a single quiddity. This unified consideration is the essential predicated. The author then clarifies the meaning of such predication: when we say 'Animal is man,' the two concepts are distinct in the mind but their external or estimative identity is one. Therefore, neither the unity of two things nor predication of a thing of itself follows. This resolves the objection that if predicate and subject are different, predication would require their unity; if identical, it would be self-predication. The author's explanation shows that the predication is real because it is based on the identity of the referent despite conceptual distinction.

Correction: The passage shows that predicate and subject are distinct in the mind but identical in external reality, so predication is neither conflation nor self-predication.

Key Takeaway

The Three Probabilities Regarding Predicated Parts
Sanusi enumerates three probabilities for distinct predicated concepts of a quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible explanations for how multiple distinct concepts can be predicated of a single quiddity.

Core Idea
The quiddity's predicated parts force three possibilities: one simple thing, multiple things with multiple existences, or multiple things with one existence.

Sanusi has distinguished between external parts (like body parts) and predicated parts (like genus and differentia). The real theological difficulty is composition from predicated parts, which are mutually true of one another. In this passage, he lays out the three and only three logical possibilities for how these distinct mental concepts relate to the quiddity itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice that the author distinguishes external parts from predicated parts. The difficulty is only with predicated parts, like 'animal' and 'man' which are true of each other.
This problem has perplexed earlier thinkers.
2
Distinguish essential and accidental concepts
Some concepts predicated of man are accidental (walker, writer) and some are essential (substance, animal). Focus on the essential ones that are not external.
Essential concepts are distinct in the mind.
3
Learn the three probabilities
The essential distinct images must be either (1) images of one simple thing, (2) images of multiple things with multiple existences, or (3) images of multiple things with one existence.
Each probability has proponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا المقام يستدعي مزيد بسط في الكلام لينضبط به المرام، وهو أن تقول: لا إشكال في تركب الماهية من الأجزاء الخارجية التي لا تحمل عليها مواطأة إنما الإشكال في تركبها من الأجزاء المحمولة عليها المتصادقة بعضها على بعض ولذلك تحيرت فيها الأوهام واختلفت المذاهب، ووجه ضبطها أن يقال: ماهية الإنسان مثلا يصدق عليها مفهومات متعددة كالجوهر والجسم والحيوان، وكالماشي والكاتب والضاحك إلى غير ذلك، وليست نسبة هذه المفهومات إلى الماهية الإنسانية على السوية، بل بعضها خارجة عنها عارضة لها كالماشي وأخواته وبعضها ليست كذلك كالجوهر وأخواته. ثم إن هذه المفهومات التي ليست خارجة عنها لا شك أنها متغايرة في الذهن بحسب أنفسها ووجوداتها أيضاً، فهذه الصور المتغايرة في الذهن إما أن تكون صوراً لشيء واحد في حد ذاته بسيط لا تعدد فيه أو تكون صوراً لأشياء متعددة متغايرة الماهية وعلى الثاني إما أن تكون تلك الماهية المتعددة موجودة بوجودات متعددة، أو بوجود واحد، فهذه احتمالات ثلاثة لا مزيد عليها، وقد ذهب إلى كل واحد منها طائفة: الاحتمال الأول: أن تكون تلك الصور لشيء واحد هو بسيط ذاتاً
This place requires more expansion in speech to control the aim. It is that you say: There is no difficulty in the composition of the quiddity from external parts that are not predicated of it by correspondence; the difficulty is in its composition from the predicated parts that are true of each other. That is why minds have been perplexed about it and schools have differed. The way to control it is to say: The quiddity of man, for example, is true of multiple concepts such as substance, body, animal, and such as walker, writer, laugher, etc. The relation of these concepts to the human quiddity is not equal; rather, some of them are external to it, accidental to it, like walker and its sisters, and some are not so, like substance and its sisters. Then these concepts that are not external to it are undoubtedly distinct in the mind in themselves and in their existences as well. So these distinct images in the mind are either images of a single thing that is simple in itself with no multiplicity, or they are images of multiple things with distinct quiddities. And on the second [hypothesis], either those multiple quiddities exist with multiple existences, or with a single existence. These are three probabilities with no more. A group has gone to each one of them. The first probability: that these images are of a single thing that is essentially simple.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage identifies the real problem and presents three possible solutions. The author first distinguishes between two types of parts: external parts (like parts of a body) and predicated parts (like genus and differentia). There is no problem with composition from external parts, but the difficulty lies in composition from predicated parts that are mutually true of each other (e.g., animal and man). This difficulty has caused confusion and differing schools. The author then proposes a way to control it: take the quiddity of man, which is true of multiple concepts. Some concepts are accidental (like walker), while others are essential (like substance). The essential concepts are distinct in the mind. There are three possibilities: (1) these distinct mental images are images of a single simple thing (i.e., the quiddity itself is simple, and the concepts are different mental aspects of the same simple reality). (2) They are images of multiple distinct quiddities that exist with multiple existences. (3) They are images of multiple distinct quiddities that exist with a single existence. Each probability has its proponents. The passage ends by stating the first probability: that all images are of a single simple thing.

الأجزاء المحمولة
المتصادقة
الماهية
الصور المتغايرة
الاحتمالات الثلاثة
بسيط ذاتاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All concepts predicated of a quiddity are accidental and external to it.
Correction: Some concepts (like substance and animal for man) are essential and not external; they constitute the quiddity's definition.

Checkpoint

C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The quiddity's essential predicated parts are distinct in the mind, but they can only be explained by one of three logical possibilities regarding their relationship to the quiddity itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think about your own identity—your roles, traits, and experiences—do you feel they are all aspects of one simple self, or do they feel like multiple separate parts? How does your answer affect the way you live and make decisions?
?

Parts Identical to the Composite
Defends the identity of parts and composite in essence and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that parts are identical to the composite in external existence and essence, and how objections concerning the natural universal and metaphorical union are refuted.

Core Idea
Parts are not distinct from the composite; they are the same reality viewed mentally.

This passage presents and defends the first of four possibilities for the relationship between parts and a composite: they are identical in both essence and existence externally, differing only in the mind. The author explains why this is the chosen position of the verified scholars and addresses key objections, especially those of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani concerning the natural universal and the nature of union.

Guided Path
1
Read the core thesis
Identify the main claim: parts are identical to the composite in essence and existence externally. Note that distinction is only mental.
Look for the phrase 'عين المركب في الخارج ماهية ووجوداً'.
2
Understand the objection about the natural universal
Al-Dawani objects that denying the natural universal means parts don't exist externally, so they can't be identical. See how the author distinguishes between parts as different from essence vs. identical to essence.
The refutation hinges on what is negated: the natural universal as a separate quiddity.
3
Examine the second objection and the analogy
Al-Dawani also suggests the union might be metaphorical and raises the issue of separate entities like God. The author answers with the genus-differentia-individual analogy and the silver-ring example.
The analogy shows how one reality can have multiple aspects with different effects.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by directing to a previous answer about how multiple intellectual forms correspond to one simple thing. Consider how this reinforces the identity thesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد العاشر : وجوب حاجة بعض الأجزاء إلى بعض ووجوداً، لكن ينتزع العقل منه باعتبارات شتى هذه الصور المتخالفة كما مر، وهذا هو القول بأن الأجزاء المحمولة عين المركب في الخارج ماهية ووجوداً، وأن جعل الأجزاء في الخارج هو بعينه جعل المركب فيه ولا امتياز بينهما إلا في الذهن، وهو المختار عند المحققين كما بين في الكتاب ولا إشكال عليه إلا ما سلف أن من احتمالات أربعة، فمبني على أنه أراد بكونه صوراً لأمر واحد أن يكون مطابقاً له مرآة لمشاهدة أمر واحد ، وإلا فتلك الأمور المتعددة إن كانت داخلة في ماهية ذلك الواحد كان داخلا في القسم الثاني، وإن كانت خارجة عنه لم تكن أجزاء .

قوله : ( أن تكون تلك الصور لشيء واحد بسيط أي بالقياس إلى تلك الصور، فلا ينافي ذلك تركب ذاته، ولذا قال : لا تعدد فيه، فعلى هذا يكون التركيب منها في العقل فقط .

قوله : ( باعتبارات شتى من تنبه المشاركات والمباينات كما مر .

قوله: (ولا) امتیاز بينهما إلخ تفسير للعينية يعني لما كانت منتزعة من نفس الهوية البسيطة من غير ملاحظة أمر آخر وجودي أو سلبي ولم يكن بينهما امتياز في الخارج الخارج لا من حيث الماهية، ولا من حيث الوجود، كانت عينها وجعلها جعلها .

وأما ما قاله المحقق الدواني : من أن أصحاب هذا المذهب ينفون وجود الكلي الطبيعي، فتلك الأجزاء غير موجودة في الخارج، فلا تكون عين المركب في الخارج ومتحدة معه في الجعل ففيه أنهم إنما ينفون وجود الكلي الطبيعي بأن يكون أمراً مغايراً للذات ماهية، فاللازم منه أن لا تكون الأجزاء من حيث مغايرتها للذات موجودة في الخارج، وذلك لا ينافي وجودها من حيث إنها عين الذات في الخارج.

قوله : ( ولا إشكال فيه إلا ما سلف إلخ قال المحقق الدواني فيه أشياء أخر مثل أن يكون الحكم باتحادهما مجازياً من قبيل اتحاد المعدوم بالموجود في الوجود لعلاقة بينهما، وأن تكون المفارقات كالواجب تعالى والماهيات المركبة المادية من الإنسان وغيره أجيب بأن مبدأ الصورتين متحقق في الثانية بلا تمايز وتعدد في الوجود ،والجعل بخلاف الأولى فإن من قال باتحاد الأجزاء بالمركب ذاتاً ووجوداً لم يرد به نفي المبادئ بالكلية، بل تحقيق كلامهم أن الآثار الجنسبة مبدؤها الجنس كما أن الفصلية مبدؤها الفصل، لكن تحصل المبدأ الأول بالفصل كما أن تعين الثاني وتشخصه بوجود الشخص، فلم يكن لها وجودات متعددة وذوات متخالفة، بل إنما صارت ذات الجنس متحصلا بالفصل، وذات الفصل هو بعينه ذات الشخص. فغاية الأمر أن مادة مبهمة مسماة بالجنس تعينت وصارت بهذا التعين مسماة بالفصل ثم تشخصت فصارت شخصاً، كما أن مادة الفضة مثلاً إذا أخذت بوصف الفضة تكون مبهمة بالقياس إلى الصور التي قابلة لها، وإذا أخذت معها صورة الخاتم تحصلت وزال إبهامها الكائن في حد نفسها فإذا وجد منها شخص اتحد الفضة والخاتم والشخص منه ذاتاً ووجوداً، أن هناك فضة وخاتماً وشخصاً وآثاراً مترتبة على الفضة كالتقوية والتفريح للقلب، وعلى الخاتم من التزيين، وعلى الشخص والهوية من الرزانة والشغل للحيز مع أنه خاتم في نفسه.

الصور العقلية المختلفة كيف يتصور مطابقتها لأمر واحد بسيط في الخارج؟ وقد عرفت جوابه هناك.
The second observatory - the tenth purpose: The necessity of some parts needing others in existence, but the intellect extracts from it, with different considerations, these differing forms as has passed. This is the statement that the predicated parts are identical to the composite in external existence, both in essence and existence, and that the making of the parts externally is exactly the making of the composite in it, and there is no distinction between them except in the mind. This is the chosen position of the verified scholars as explained in the book, and there is no problem with it except what has preceded, that among four possibilities, it is based on the idea that by being forms of one thing, he intended it to be corresponding to it, a mirror for observing one thing. Otherwise, those multiple things, if they were included in the essence of that one, would be part of the second category, and if they were outside it, they would not be parts. His statement: 'That those forms are for a simple one thing' i.e., in relation to those forms, so the composition of its essence does not contradict that, hence he said: no multiplicity in it, and thus the composition from them is only in the mind. His statement: 'With different considerations such as noting the shared aspects and differences as has passed.' His statement: 'No distinction between them etc.' is an explanation of identity, meaning, since they are abstracted from the same simple identity without observing another existential or negative matter, and there was no distinction between them in external externality, neither in terms of essence nor existence, they are its identical and their making is its making. As for what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said: that the adherents of this school deny the existence of the natural universal, so those parts do not exist externally, so they cannot be identical to the composite externally and united with it in making; in response: they only deny the existence of the natural universal as something different from the essence in quiddity, so the consequence is that the parts, in terms of their difference from the essence, do not exist externally, and that does not contradict their existence in terms of being identical to the essence externally. His statement: 'There is no problem except what has preceded etc.' Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said there are other things, such as the judgment of their union being metaphorical, like the union of the non-existent with the existent in existence due to a relation between them, and that the separate entities like the Most High Necessary and the material composite essences like man and others; it was answered that the principle of the two forms is realized in the second without differentiation and multiplicity in existence and making, unlike the first. For whoever says that the parts are united with the composite in essence and existence does not intend thereby the total negation of the principles; rather, the verification of their statement is that the generic effects have their principle in the genus just as the specific effects have their principle in the differentia, but the first principle is actualized by the differentia just as the determination of the second and its individuation are by the existence of the individual. So they do not have multiple existences and differing essences; rather, the essence of the genus becomes actualized by the differentia, and the essence of the differentia is exactly the essence of the individual. So the utmost is that an ambiguous matter called genus becomes determined and by this determination becomes called differentia, then becomes individuated and becomes an individual. Just as, for example, silver matter when taken with the description of silver is ambiguous in relation to the forms it can receive, and when taken with the form of the ring it becomes actualized and its ambiguity which was in its own limits disappears, so when an individual of it exists, the silver, the ring, and the individual from it are united in essence and existence; that there is a silver, a ring, and an individual, and effects arranged upon the silver such as strengthening and gladdening the heart, upon the ring such as adornment, and upon the individual and identity such as gravity and occupying space while being a ring in itself. The different intellectual forms: how can their correspondence to a single simple thing externally be imagined? And you have known the answer there.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the first possibility regarding the relationship between parts and the composite. It begins by stating that the chosen view is that the parts are identical to the composite externally in both essence and existence; the distinction between them is only mental. The author then explains that any problem with this view is based on a misunderstanding of what it means for forms to represent a single thing. He then provides commentary on specific phrases, clarifying that the composition is only in the mind. The bulk of the passage addresses an objection by al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani: that proponents of this view deny the natural universal, so parts cannot be identical to the composite externally. The refutation explains that the negation of the natural universal only applies to parts considered as different from the essence, not when they are identical to the essence in existence. Al-Dawani raises further objections: that the union might be metaphorical, or that separate entities like God and material composites are different. The answer uses the analogy of genus, differentia, and individual, and illustrates with the example of silver and a ring: the silver, ring, and individual are one in essence and existence, yet different aspects have different effects. The passage concludes by noting that the answer to how different intellectual forms correspond to one simple thing has already been given.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying the natural universal means parts have no external reality at all.
Correction: The denial applies only to parts considered as distinct from the essence, not when they are identical to the essence in existence.

Checkpoint
According to the first possibility defended in this passage, what is the relationship between the parts and the composite in external existence?
A) The parts are distinct from the composite in both essence and existence.
B) The parts are identical to the composite in both essence and existence.
C) The parts are identical in essence but distinct in existence.
D) The parts are distinct in essence but identical in existence.

Key Takeaway
Parts and composite are one reality externally; their multiplicity is only in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the multiplicity of parts in a composite is only in the mind, while externally they are one reality. In your own life, do you experience a similar division within yourself—between your body, spirit, intellect, and desires? Are you living as if these are separate, conflicting parts, or have you realized they are all aspects of one integrated self that must answer to Allah? What can you do today to align your inner multiplicity with the oneness of your servitude to Him?
?
This lesson argues that effects (like those of genus and differentia) belong to the same existent without any real separation. Consider your own actions: Do you sometimes attribute good deeds to one 'part' of yourself (e.g., your knowledge) and bad deeds to another (e.g., your weakness), as if they come from different sources? How does the truth that you are a single, indivisible soul before Allah affect your accountability for every action?
?
The analogy of silver, ring, and individual shows that one thing can have multiple descriptions and effects. Think about your identity: you are a Muslim, a son/daughter, a student, a citizen, etc. Do you sometimes treat these roles as separate, competing identities? How can you see them as different aspects of the same servitude to Allah, each with its own effects, yet all united in your single purpose of worship?
